Maturidi's Refutation of Mu'tazili Interpretation of Verse 49:9
Maturidi offers two interpretations of verse 49:9 to refute Mu'tazili claims.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi refutes the Mu'tazili claim that the verse about fighting believers implies they cease to be believers.

Core Idea
The verse 'If two parties of believers fight' itself affirms their belief.

In this passage, Maturidi addresses a key Mu'tazili argument that uses verse 49:9 to deny the faith of major sinners. He provides two interpretations that preserve the believer status of the fighters and demonstrates that the command to reconcile and the use of 'brothers' nullifies any notion of apostasy. This lesson unpacks his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once, then read the translation. Notice the structure of the argument.
2
Identify the two interpretations
Maturidi gives two possible explanations for the fighting in the verse. Find them and understand how each one preserves the believer status.
3
Analyze the conclusion
See how the command to fight the transgressing party actually confirms they are still believers, not apostates.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم أجاب في قوله : " وَإِنَّ طَائِفَتَانِ مِنَ المُؤْمِنِينَ اقْتَتَلُوا " أنه كقوله : وَمَنْ يَرْتَدِدْ مِنْكُمْ عَنْ دِينِهِ وقد كان سماه مؤمنا من قبل ، والثانى أن يكون الاقتتال بغير سلاح نحو المجاذبة أو كانوا مجتهدين فلا يخرجون به من الإيمان . فيقال : إذ جرى الأمر بالإصلاح بينهم وتسمية الإخوة بطل معنى الردة ، وقوله : فَقاتِلُوا الَّتي تَبْغِي ، دل أن الباغى كان معلوما ، لا أن كان ثمة ما كان رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم بين أظهرهم أبي لهم الاجتهاد مع أن ذنوبهم إلى ذلك الحد ، ثم دل الأمر بالقتال والصغيرة تكون مغفورة لا يقابل عليها ، على قد كبرت وقد أبقى الله لهم اسم الإيمان ، والله الموفق وقال في قوله : إِنَّمَا المُؤْمِنُونَ إخوَةٌ ، بمثل ذلك ، وقد بينا وهمه . ثم على قوله إن صاحب الكبيرة عدو الله لا يسع له الدعاء بالخير ويلزم لعنه ، ومنا الإصلاح إلا الدعاء بالخير والصلاح ، ولا قوة إلا بالله
Then he answered regarding His statement: 'And if two parties among the believers fight' that it is like His statement: 'And whoever among you turns back from his religion' and He had called him a believer before. And the second is that the fighting was without weapons, like pulling and tugging, or they were mujtahids, so they do not exit from faith thereby. So it is said: Since the command for reconciliation between them and the naming of brotherhood occurred, the meaning of apostasy is invalidated. And His statement: 'Fight the one that transgresses' indicates that the transgressor was known, not that there was any ijtihad while the Messenger of Allah was among them — He prevented them from ijtihad, even though their sins reached that limit. Then the command to fight indicates that even if the sin is great, they have kept the name of faith. And Allah is the Grantor of success. And He said regarding His statement: 'Indeed the believers are brothers' similarly, and we have shown his error. Then regarding his statement that the committer of a major sin is an enemy of God, it is not permissible to pray for good for him and he must be cursed, while from us [the proper conduct] is nothing but prayer for good and rectification. And there is no power except in Allah.
Source pages p. 957

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazili position that major sinners do not retain the name 'believer'. Maturidi first addresses their use of verse 49:9 ('if two parties of believers fight'). He gives two possible interpretations: either the fighting is like apostasy (but the verse calls them believers, so it is not apostasy), or the fighting is without weapons (thus not excommunicating). He then argues that the command to reconcile and the continued use of 'brotherhood' invalidates any claim of apostasy. The command to fight the transgressing party shows that the transgressor is still a believer, and the concept of ijtihad is irrelevant when the Prophet was present. Then, regarding verse 49:10 ('believers are brothers'), he says the same reasoning applies. Finally, he condemns the Mu'tazili view that major sinners are enemies of God who must be cursed; instead, Muslims should pray for their good and rectification.

Key Terms
إيمان
مؤمن
كبیرة
ردة
اجتهاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Fighting among believers means they are no longer believers.
Correction: The verse itself calls them believers, and Maturidi shows that fighting does not strip the name of faith.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi's answer in this passage, what does the command 'Fight the one that transgresses' (فقاتلوا التي تبغي) indicate about the transgressing party?
A) They have apostatized from Islam
B) They are still known as believers despite their transgression
C) They are not considered Muslims
D) They must be killed without reconciliation

Key Takeaway
Maturidi demonstrates that verse 49:9 actually preserves the title of believer for those who fight, countering the Mu'tazili argument.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see fellow believers committing grave sins or even fighting one another, do you still genuinely consider them your brothers in faith? What would change in your treatment of them if you internalized Maturidi's reasoning that the title 'believer' remains despite major sins?
?
In your own life, have you ever judged another Muslim so harshly for a sin that you mentally stripped them of the name 'believer'? What would it mean for you to extend the same grace that the verse does, while still commanding what is right?
?
If you were among a group of believers in a heated dispute, how would Maturidi's second interpretation—that the fight might be a scuffle or a matter of ijtihad—affect the way you approach reconciliation? Will you actively work to preserve the bonds of brotherhood with those you disagree with, starting this week?

The Status of Major Sinners and Prohibition of Cursing
Maturidi rejects cursing major sinners and advocates prayer for their rectification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi rejects the Mu'tazili view that major sinners are enemies of God and must be cursed.

Core Idea
Major sinners remain believers and should be prayed for, not cursed.

After refuting the Mu'tazili interpretation of verse 49:9, Maturidi turns to their broader claim that committers of major sins are enemies of God deserving cursing. He contrasts this with the proper Muslim conduct of praying for good and seeking rectification. This lesson covers his condemnation of that extreme view.

Guided Path
1
Read the short passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the key claim and rebuttal.
2
Identify the Mu'tazili position
The Mu'tazilis held that major sinners are enemies of God and must be cursed. Find where Maturidi states this.
3
Contrast with proper conduct
Maturidi states that the proper conduct is only prayer for good and rectification. Understand why this is the correct stance.

Source Text

Brotherhood in Religion and the Name 'Believer'
Maturidi refutes the Mu'tazili interpretation of brotherhood in the qisas verse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the verse of retaliation to argue that major sinners retain the name 'believer' and that brotherhood refers to religious brotherhood.

Core Idea
The name 'believer' is not removed by sin; brotherhood in religion remains.

Maturidi confronts the Mu'tazili interpretation of the verse of retaliation (qisas). They claimed that the term 'brotherhood' in that verse refers to absolute brotherhood, not religious. Maturidi counters by pointing out that the verse itself calls them believers at the start, and then preserves the name of brotherhood at the end. This shows that the brotherhood is religious, and the name 'believer' is maintained. Let's examine his argument closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how Maturidi sets up the Mu'tazili claim and then refutes it.
Pay attention to the structure of the verse he quotes.
2
Identify the Mu'tazili claim
The Mu'tazila argued that the brotherhood mentioned in the verse of qisas is absolute, not religious, and thus the verse does not prove that major sinners are believers. Find that claim in the passage.
3
Analyze Maturidi's response
Maturidi points out that the verse first calls them 'believers' and then calls them 'brothers'. Since the context is religious, the brotherhood must be religious. Understand the logical sequence.
He says there is no prior meaning for the word 'brotherhood' to be assigned to.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider the broader point: the name 'believer' is not negated by major sins. The verse maintains both the name of faith and the name of brotherhood in religion.
This is a key Maturidi position against the Khawarij and Mu'tazila.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال فى آية القصاص وما فيه من تسمية الإخوة : إن الله لم يعد على الإخوة المطلقة ثوابا ولا مدحا ، وإنما كان ذلك فى الإخوة في الدين . فيقال لهم : قد سماهم مؤمنين فى أول الآية ثم أبقى لهم اسم الإخوة في آخرها ، ولا معنى سبق يحتمل حرف ذكر الإخوة إليه ، ثبت أنه في الدين مع إبقاء اسم الإيمان
وأما الثواب فقد شرط مرة باسم المطلق ومرة باسم المقيد ، من ذلك قوله : ( فَأَثَابَهُم اللهُ بِمَا قالوا ، ثم قد يجوز عندك التحذير مع وجود القول وإن وعد عليه الثواب، فمثله المؤمن باسم الإطلاق . وقال : وَالَّذينَ آمَنُوا بِاللَّهِ وَرَسُلِهِ أُولَئِكَ هُم الصَّدِّيقُونَ )، وكذلك قوله : وَالَّذِينَ آمَنُوا بِاللَّهِ وَرَسُلِهِ وَلَمْ يُفَرِّقُوا بَيْنَ أَحَدٍ ، وصاحب الكبيرة يقال آمن بالله ورسله ولم يفرق بين أحد من رسله ، جائز في مثله التخويف والوعيد ، ولذلك قال الله : وَإِن تَكُ حَسَنَةٌ يُضَاعِفُهَا ويوت من لَّدُنْهُ أَجْرًا عَظِيمًا ، وصاحب الكبيرة قد أتى بحسنة ويستحق الذي جاء به اسم الحسنة فما تنكر أن يستحق اسم المؤمن وإن أوعد ، ولا قوة إلا بالله
And He said regarding the verse of retaliation and what it contains of naming brotherhood: 'Indeed Allah did not promise to absolute brotherhood any reward nor praise, but that was for brotherhood in religion.' So it is said to them: 'He called them believers at the beginning of the verse, then kept for them the name of brotherhood at its end, and there is no prior meaning that the word 'brotherhood' could be assigned to, so it is established that it is in religion along with keeping the name of faith.'
As for reward, He sometimes conditioned it with the absolute name and sometimes with the restricted name. Among that is His statement: 'So Allah rewarded them for what they said.' Then it may be permissible in your view to warn despite the existence of the statement even though He promised reward upon it. Similarly, the believer is [called] by the absolute name. And He said: 'And those who believe in Allah and His messengers — those are the truthful ones (siddiqun).' And likewise His statement: 'And those who believe in Allah and His messengers and do not differentiate between any of them.' The committer of a major sin is said to believe in Allah and His messengers and not differentiate between any of His messengers. The threat and warning are permissible in his case. That is why Allah said: 'And if there is a good deed, He multiplies it and gives from Himself a great reward.' The committer of a major sin has performed a good deed, and the name 'good deed' is deserved for what he brought. So why do you deny that he deserves the name 'believer' even if he is threatened? And there is no power except in Allah.
Source pages p. 957

Commentary
This passage contains two distinct arguments. First, Maturidi refutes the Mu'tazili claim that the verse of retaliation (qisas) and the naming of brotherhood therein refers only to religious brotherhood, not absolute. He replies that the verse calls them believers at the start and preserves the name of brotherhood, so it is clearly religious brotherhood along with maintaining the name of faith. Second, he discusses reward (thawab): sometimes Allah conditions reward upon the absolute name 'believer', sometimes upon restricted descriptions (e.g., 'those who believe and do good'). He cites verses like 'So Allah rewarded them for what they said' (5:85) and 'those are the truthful ones' (57:19) and 'those who believe in Allah and His messengers and do not differentiate' (4:158). A major sinner still believes in Allah and His messengers and does not differentiate, so he can be warned and threatened, but he also has good deeds. Since a good deed earns the name 'good', he deserves the name 'believer' even if threatened. Thus, the name 'mu'min' is not negated by the threat of punishment.

Key Terms
قصاص
إخوة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How does Maturidi establish that the brotherhood in the verse of retaliation is religious brotherhood?
A) By noting that the verse promises reward for brotherhood
B) By pointing out that the verse calls them believers at the beginning and keeps the name of brotherhood at the end
C) By citing a different verse about brotherhood
D) By arguing that all humans are brothers

Key Takeaway
The name 'believer' remains for the major sinner, and brotherhood in religion is affirmed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever mentally excluded someone from the brotherhood of Islam because of a sin you considered too great? How does Maturidi's insistence that the name 'believer' remains for the sinner challenge your heart to see them as a brother still in need of your care?
?
If you truly believed that no sin removes the name 'believer' from a person, how would it change the way you speak about Muslims who fall into major sins? Would you still be quick to label them or distance yourself?
?
The Mu'tazila argued that the opening address 'O you who have believed' was only for the original audience, not for later sinners. In your own life, do you treat the title 'believer' as conditional—something you earn by being sinless? Or do you accept it as a gift that remains despite failure? What would it mean for your relationship with Allah if you truly believed that?

Reward, Good Deeds, and Retaining the Name 'Believer'
Maturidi argues that major sinners deserve the name 'believer' based on reward verses and the analogy of good deeds.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses verses about reward and good deeds to argue that major sinners deserve the name 'believer' despite the threat of punishment.

Core Idea
A good deed earns the name 'good'; faith earns the name 'believer' despite threats.

After addressing the verse of retaliation, Maturidi turns to the topic of reward (thawab). He observes that Allah sometimes conditions reward on the absolute name of faith, and sometimes on restricted descriptions. A major sinner has both faith (believing in Allah and His messengers without differentiation) and good deeds. Therefore, just as a good deed earns the name 'good', faith earns the name 'believer'—even if he is warned. Let's trace his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation for this section. Notice how Maturidi cites multiple verses.
The verses are from Quran 5:85, 57:19, 4:158, and 4:40 or similar.
2
Understand the two ways reward is conditioned
Maturidi says reward is sometimes conditioned on the absolute name (e.g., 'believer') and sometimes on restricted descriptions (e.g., 'those who believe and do good').
He uses the verse 'So Allah rewarded them for what they said' as an example of absolute.
3
Analyze the example of the siddiqun
The verse 'Those who believe in Allah and His messengers are the siddiqun' includes all believers, even major sinners, because they believe and do not differentiate.
The major sinner meets the condition of that verse.
4
Apply the good deed analogy
Allah says 'If there is a good deed, He multiplies it.' A major sinner has good deeds, so he deserves the name 'good deed'. Similarly, he deserves the name 'believer' because he has faith.
The threat of punishment does not negate the reality of faith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And He said regarding the verse of retaliation and what it contains of naming brotherhood: 'Indeed Allah did not promise to absolute brotherhood any reward nor praise, but that was for brotherhood in religion.' So it is said to them: 'He called them believers at the beginning of the verse, then kept for them the name of brotherhood at its end, and there is no prior meaning that the word 'brotherhood' could be assigned to, so it is established that it is in religion along with keeping the name of faith.'

Then it may be permissible in your view to warn despite the existence of the statement even though He promised reward upon it. Similarly, the believer is [called] by the absolute name. And He said: 'And those who believe in Allah and His messengers — those are the truthful ones (siddiqun).' And likewise His statement: 'And those who believe in Allah and His messengers and do not differentiate between any of them.' The committer of a major sin is said to believe in Allah and His messengers and not differentiate between any of His messengers. The threat and warning are permissible in his case. That is why Allah said: 'And if there is a good deed, He multiplies it and gives from Himself a great reward.' The committer of a major sin has performed a good deed, and the name 'good deed' is deserved for what he brought. So why do you deny that he deserves the name 'believer' even if he is threatened? And there is no power except in Allah.

Commentary
This passage contains two distinct arguments. First, Maturidi refutes the Mu'tazili claim that the verse of retaliation (qisas) and the naming of brotherhood therein refers only to religious brotherhood, not absolute. He replies that the verse calls them believers at the start and preserves the name of brotherhood, so it is clearly religious brotherhood along with maintaining the name of faith. Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the threat of punishment (wa'id) implies the person is not a believer.
Correction: Maturidi argues that threat does not negate the name 'believer'; the major sinner still has faith and good deeds, so he is a believer.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why does a major sinner still deserve the name 'believer' despite the threat of punishment?
A) Because he never actually committed a major sin
B) Because he believes in Allah and His messengers without differentiation and has good deeds

Key Takeaway
The name 'believer' is retained for major sinners because they still possess faith and good deeds, even if they are threatened with punishment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that your faith and good deeds remain valid even when you fall into major sins? What keeps you from despairing of Allah's mercy, or from taking His threat lightly?
?
Imagine you committed a major sin today. By the logic of this passage, you would still be called a believer. How does that make you feel: relieved or uneasy? Does it encourage you to repent or to continue sinning? Be honest.
?
This lesson argues that the name 'believer' is retained despite threats. How does that affect your view of a Muslim who openly sins? Do you still consider them a brother/sister in faith? What obligation does that place on you?

Does the Name 'Iman' Determine Rights? Maturidi's Answer Using Consensus
Maturidi raises a self-objection and answers using consensus and general Qur'anic address.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi refutes the idea that legal rights require the name of faith, using consensus and the general address of Qur'anic verses.

Core Idea
Inclusion in legal rights is by consensus, not by the name of faith.

Maturidi anticipates a potential objection: if legal rights are tied to the name 'iman', then major sinners should not have those rights because they are not fully believers. He answers by showing that their inclusion comes from consensus (ijma') and the general address of Qur'anic obligations, not from the specific name. Let's see how he argues this crucial point.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Let the argument's flow come through before analyzing.
Notice the dialectical structure: objection, answer, rejoinder, final reply.
2
Identify the self-objection and answer
Find where Maturidi states the objection about legal rights, and then his answer: inclusion is by consensus, not the name.
The objection is in the first sentence: the rights are tied to the name 'iman', so major sinners should be excluded.
3
Understand the analogy with 'muttaqin'
See how Maturidi draws an analogy with verses that say 'an obligation upon the righteous' – these include major sinners despite the specific term 'muttaqin'.
The analogy shows that inclusion does not require the exact name.
4
Follow the rebuttal and final point
The opponent argues consensus alone is insufficient; Maturidi replies that consensus derives from understanding the general address of the verses. Pay attention to his explanation of 'haqqan 'ala'.
The final sentence ties the argument together, showing the name is not necessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم عارض نفسه بالحقوق التي أوجبت باسم الإيمان وأحلت به وقد دخل في ذلك أصحاب الكبائر . فأجاب بأن إدخالهم بالإجماع لا بالاسم ، كما أدخلتم في قوله : حَقًّا عَلَى المُتَّقِينَ، حَقًّا عَلَى الْمُحْسِنِينَ ، وإن لم يكن الفاسق كذلك . قيل له : الإجماع أدخلهم فى ذلك بالفهم من الخطاب بالإيجاب والتحليل بالآيات ؛ إذ ليس أحد منهم ذكر وجها به عرفوا سواه ، ولا أحد من متعاطى الفسق سأل أحدا عن خاص بل عرف تضمنه تلك الآيات ، ولم يجز الخطاب بالوجهين باسم التقوى ؛ لذلك بطل التقدير . وقوله : حق على كذا، أي حق على من يريد التقوى ذلك وليس فيه إيجاب ، مع ما كان في الذي يذكر تخصيص معنى التقوى في حق الخطاب فيما نحن فيه يدخله في الخطاب بلا اسم الذى به خوطب لا بالإطلاق ولا بتخصيص ، ولا قوة إلا بالله
Then he objected to himself regarding the rights that are made obligatory by the name of faith and made permissible by it, and the committers of major sins enter into that. So he answered: 'Their inclusion is by consensus (ijma'), not by the name, just as you include [them] in His statement: 'An obligation upon the righteous (muttaqin), an obligation upon the doers of good (muhsinin)' even if the immoral person is not like that.' It was said to him: 'Consensus included them in that by understanding from the address of obligation and permission from the verses; for none of them mentioned a way by which they were known otherwise, and none of those engaging in immorality asked anyone about a specific [ruling] but rather knew that it was included in those verses. And the address is not valid with the two aspects by the name 'taqwa'; therefore the estimation is invalidated. And His statement: 'haqqan 'ala kadha' means a right upon whoever wants taqwa of that, and it does not contain obligation, along with what is in the one who mentions specifying the meaning of taqwa in the context of address regarding what we are in — it includes him in the address without the name by which he was addressed, neither by absolute nor by specification. And there is no power except in Allah.
Source pages p. 958

Commentary
This passage is a self-objection (mu'arada) where Maturidi raises a potential counterargument: the legal rights and duties that are established 'by the name of faith' (e.g., inheritance, marriage, etc.) include major sinners, which seems to require that they are called believers by name. He answers that their inclusion is based on consensus (ijma'), not on the name itself. He analogizes with verses that say 'haqqan 'ala al-muttaqin' (an obligation upon the righteous) and 'haqqan 'ala al-muhsinin' (an obligation upon the doers of good), which include all believers, including major sinners, even though the term 'muttaqin' is specific. The opponent then points out that consensus alone cannot establish inclusion without textual basis. Maturidi replies that consensus derives from understanding the general address of the verses: the verses of obligation and permission are understood to apply to all believers, including major sinners, because the verses do not specify otherwise, and people do not ask about specific application. The term 'haqqan 'ala' does not imply obligation but rather a due right upon those who intend taqwa. Thus, the objection fails, and the name 'mu'min' is not necessary for the inclusion in legal rights.

إجماع
متقون
محسنون
تقوى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If legal rights apply to major sinners, they must be called 'believers' by name.
Correction: Maturidi clarifies that inclusion is via consensus and the general address of Qur'anic verses, not the specific name 'iman'.

Checkpoint
How does Maturidi answer the self-objection that legal rights (huquq) are tied to the name 'iman' and include major sinners?
A) He denies that major sinners are included in those rights
B) He says inclusion is by consensus (ijma'), not by the name
C) He argues that major sinners are not believers at all
D) He says the rights are only for righteous believers

Key Takeaway
Maturidi shows that legal rights for major sinners are established by consensus and the general address of the Qur'an, not by the specific name of faith.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Will you actively remind yourself of this consensus next time you judge?
?
Do you rely on the name 'Muslim' as a comfortable identity, or do you understand that your standing before Allah and the community is based on the general address of the Qur'an and the consensus of the ummah? If your faith were tested publicly, would you still see yourself as included even without the name? What needs to change in your heart to live by this reality?

The Major Sinner's Name: Mu'min Despite Sin
Maturidi discusses reward conditioned on absolute vs restricted name and the status of the major sinner.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that the committer of major sins retains the name 'believer' (mu'min) based on the distinction between absolute and restricted naming in the Quran.

Core Idea
Major sins do not strip a believer of the name 'mu'min'—the Quran's absolute name of faith remains.

In this passage, Imam al-Maturidi tackles a pivotal question in Islamic theology: can someone who commits major sins still be called a believer? He turns to the Quran to show that the name 'believer' is sometimes used absolutely and sometimes restrictively. By examining how reward is conditioned in the Quran, Maturidi argues that the committer of major sins is still included under the absolute name of faith, even though he may face punishment.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the Quranic verses Maturidi cites.
Pay attention to the terms 'absolute name' and 'restricted name'.
2
Identify the two conditions of reward
Find where Maturidi says reward is conditioned by the absolute name and by the restricted name. What examples does he give?
The first verse is about reward for saying something.
3
Understand the application to the major sinner
Maturidi applies the same logic to the committer of major sins. He argues that such a person still believes in Allah and His messengers, so he deserves the absolute name 'believer'.
Why does he mention good deeds?
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider Maturidi's final question: 'So what prevents you from his deserving the name believer even though he is threatened?' This is his main point.
It counters the view that sin negates faith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الثواب فقد شرط مرة باسم المطلق ومرة باسم المقيد، من ذلك قوله: (فَأَثَابَهُم اللهُ بِمَا قالوا) ثم قد يجوز عندك التحذير مع وجود القول وإن وعد عليه الثواب، فمثله المؤمن باسم الإطلاق. وقال: (وَالَّذينَ آمَنُوا بِاللَّهِ وَرَسُلِهِ أُولَئِكَ هُم الصَّدِّيقُونَ)، وكذلك قوله: (وَالَّذِينَ آمَنُوا بِاللَّهِ وَرُسُلِهِ وَلَمْ يُفَرِّقُوا بَيْنَ أَحَدٍ) وصاحب الكبيرة يقال آمن بالله ورسله ولم يفرق بين أحد من رسله، جائز في مثله التخويف والوعيد، ولذلك قال الله: (وَإِن تَكُ حَسَنَةٌ يُضَاعِفُهَا) ويوت من لَّدُنْهُ أَجْرًا عَظِيمًا، وصاحب الكبيرة قد أتى بحسنة ويستحق الذي جاء به اسم الحسنة فما تنكر أن يستحق اسم المؤمن وإن أوعد، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
As for reward, it is conditioned sometimes by the absolute name and sometimes by the restricted name. From that is His saying: 'So Allah rewarded them for what they said.' Then it is permissible with you to threaten despite the existence of the statement even though He promised reward upon it. So likewise the believer by the absolute name. And He said: 'And those who believe in Allah and His messengers, those are the truthful ones.' And similarly His saying: 'And those who believe in Allah and His messengers and do not differentiate between any of them.' And the committer of a major sin is said to have believed in Allah and His messengers and did not differentiate between any of His messengers. Threatening and warning are permissible in such a case. Therefore Allah said: 'And if there is a good deed, He multiplies it and gives from Himself a great reward.' And the committer of a major sin has performed a good deed and deserves the name 'good deed' for what he has done. So what prevents you from his deserving the name 'believer' even though he is threatened? And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 958

Commentary
This passage discusses the theological issue of whether a person who commits major sins (kabair) can still be called a believer (mu'min). Maturidi argues that reward is sometimes attached to the absolute name of faith and sometimes to a restricted name. He cites Quranic verses to show that the committers of major sins are included under the absolute name of faith, such as in verses about believing in Allah and His messengers. He then argues that such a person can still be threatened with punishment while retaining the name of believer, just as he retains the name 'good deed' for his good actions. The key point is that the name 'mu'min' is not negated by sin, but punishment remains a possibility.

اسم المطلق
اسم المقيد
صاحب الكبيرة
مؤمن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That committing major sins removes one from faith entirely.
Correction: Maturidi argues that the name 'mu'min' is not negated by sin; the committer of major sins remains a believer in the absolute sense.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Maturidi affirms that the committer of major sins retains the name 'believer' based on the Quran's use of the absolute name of faith, while still being subject to potential punishment.

The Objection of Rights and the Answer by Consensus
Maturidi addresses the objection that major sinners are included in rights by consensus, not by name.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the principle of consensus (ijma') to resolve the apparent contradiction that major sinners are included in the legal rights of faith yet are still threatened with punishment.

Core Idea
Inclusion in legal rights is by communal consensus, not by the name of faith.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi addresses a critical objection: if major sinners are included in the rights and obligations that come with faith (like inheritance and marriage), how can they be threatened with divine punishment? He answers by distinguishing between legal inclusion by consensus and theological naming. The argument draws a parallel with Quranic phrases that address the pious and the good-doers, showing that such inclusion does not imply moral approval.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Notice the rhythm of the objection and response.
The passage uses a self-objection style common in kalam.
2
Identify the objection and the answer
Find where Maturidi raises the objection (the first sentence) and where he begins his answer. Underline the key terms: حقوق (rights), إجماع (consensus), اسم الإيمان (name of faith).
The answer uses an analogy with المتقين and المحسنين.
3
Understand the analogy
Maturidi compares the inclusion of major sinners in the rights of faith to the inclusion of people in Quranic statements like 'a right upon the pious.' Just as those statements don't require the person to be perfectly pious, the legal rights of faith don't require the name 'mu'min' to be applicable.
This shows the difference between legal rulings and theological categories.
4
Reflect on the role of consensus
Note that Maturidi says the inclusion is 'by consensus, not by the name.' This means the community's agreement grants legal rights, not the individual's designation as a believer.
Consensus (ijma') is a key source of law in Islam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم عارض نفسه بالحقوق التي أوجبت باسم الإيمان وأحلت به وقد دخل في ذلك أصحاب الكبائر. فأجاب بأن إدخالهم بالإجماع لا بالاسم، كما أدخلتم في قوله: (حَقًّا عَلَى المُتَّقِينَ)، (حَقًّا عَلَى الْمُحْسِنِينَ)، وإن لم يكن الفاسق كذلك. قيل له: الإجماع أدخلهم فى ذلك بالفهم من الخطاب بالإيجاب والتحليل بالآيات؛ إذ ليس أحد منهم ذكر وجها به عرفوا سواه، ولا أحد من متعاطى الفسق سأل / أحدا عن خاص بل عرف تضمنه تلك الآيات، ولم يجز الخطاب بالوجهين باسم التقوى؛ لذلك بطل التقدير. وقوله: حق على كذا، أي حق على من يريد التقوى ذلك وليس فيه إيجاب، مع ما كان في الذي يذكر تخصيص معنى التقوى في حق الخطاب فيما نحن فيه يدخله في الخطاب بلا اسم الذى به خوطب لا بالإطلاق ولا بتخصيص، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Then he objected to himself regarding the rights that are obligated by the name of faith and permitted by it, and the committers of major sins are included in that. So he answered that their inclusion is by consensus (ijma'), not by the name, just as you include in His saying: 'A right upon the pious,' 'A right upon the doers of good,' even though the sinner is not like that. It was said to him: Consensus included them in that by understanding from the speech of obligation and analysis by the verses, since none of them mentioned a way by which they knew otherwise, and none of those engaged in sin asked anyone about a specific, but rather they recognized its inclusion in those verses. And the speech is not valid with two aspects in the name of piety; therefore the estimation is invalid. And His saying: 'A right upon such' means a right upon whoever intends piety, and there is no obligation in it, along with what was in that which mentions the specification of the meaning of piety with regard to the speech concerning our subject, it enters him into the speech without the name by which he was addressed, neither absolutely nor by specification. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 958

Commentary
In this passage, Maturidi raises a possible objection: if major sinners are included in the rights and obligations that come with faith (such as inheritance, marriage, etc.), then how can they be threatened? He answers that their inclusion in those rights is based on consensus (ijma'), not on the name 'mu'min' itself. He draws an analogy with Quranic phrases like 'a right upon the pious' which do not necessarily mean that every pious person is obligated, but rather it is a right upon whoever intends piety. Similarly, the rights of iman are established by the community's consensus, not by the name. The objection and answer highlight the difference between legal rulings and theological names.

حقوق
إجماع
اسم الإيمان
تقوى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if major sinners are included in the legal rights of faith, then they must be considered true believers (mu'minun) in the theological sense.
Correction: Maturidi clarifies that inclusion in legal rights is based on communal consensus (ijma'), not on the name of faith itself. The legal rulings apply by agreement of the community, not because the sinner deserves the title of believer.

Checkpoint

C) By denying that major sinners have any rights
D) By saying that the Quran explicitly includes them

Key Takeaway
The inclusion of major sinners in the legal rights of faith is by communal consensus, not by their designation as believers.

The Objection of the Madman and Child and the Validity of Worship
Maturidi refutes objections about the rulings of madmen and children and the validity of worship for sinners.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi refutes objections that attempt to compare the major sinner to a madman or child, and how he establishes that acts of worship are valid only due to faith.

Core Idea
Faith alone makes worship valid, not external factors.

In this passage, Maturidi addresses two related objections. The first misapplies the rulings of madmen and children to argue that the major sinner's worship is not due to his own faith. The second questions the purpose of prayer and fasting for a sinner. Maturidi clarifies that both objections stem from a misunderstanding: acts of worship are valid only because of the presence of faith, not because of any external benefit.

Guided Path
1
Examine the first objection about the madman and child
Read the opening lines where the objector argues that the rulings for the madman and child are due to external factors, and concludes that the sinner deserves his worship by his own faith. Notice the objector's reasoning.
The objector tries to draw an analogy between the madman/child and the major sinner.
2
Analyze the second objection about prayer and fasting
Read the next part where another objection is raised: the sinner's prayer and fasting are only to prevent an increase in sin. Consider how this objection challenges the validity of the sinner's worship.
3
Understand the shaykh's clarification
Read the shaykh's response that the objector misunderstood. The acts are permitted by faith alone; without faith, they would not be valid. Reflect on how this answers both objections.
The shaykh's commentary is part of the source, so it is authoritative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعارض فى التحليل بالمجنون والصغير، قيل: لها حكم الإيمان بغيرهما؛ إذ لولا ذلك الغير لم يجب لها ذلك، كما لو لم يجب لأولاد الكفرة وما نحن فيه لا غير فى ذلك يتبعه، فثبت أنه استوجبه بإيمان نفسه. ثم عورض بما يصلى فقال: لئلا يزداد فسقه ويزول عنه عذاب تركها. قال الشيخ رحمه الله: يقال له: لم يفهم السؤال، وإنما معنى ذلك أنهما يجوزان بالإيمان، فلولا أنه مؤمن لم يكن ليمضيا له ويزيلا عذاب تركها عنه، بل لا شيء عليه في تركها، ولا يجوز أن يفعله لو لم يكن مؤمنا، ولا قوة إلا بالله. وقد أفردنا في بعض كتبنا في هذه الآيات كتابا أغنانا عن الإطناب في هذا الباب
And he objected regarding analysis with the madman and the child. It was said: The ruling of iman is for them due to other than them; for if not for that other, it would not be obligatory for them, just as it is not obligatory for the children of the disbelievers. And our subject has no other in that to follow. So it is established that he deserved it by his own iman. Then he was objected to regarding what he prayed, so he said: Lest his sin increase and the punishment of abandoning it be removed from him. The shaykh (may Allah have mercy on him) said: It is said to him: He did not understand the question. Rather the meaning is that both (prayer and fasting) are permitted by faith. For if he were not a believer, they would not be valid for him and would not remove the punishment of abandoning them from him. Rather, there would be nothing upon him for abandoning them, and it is not permissible for him to do them if he were not a believer. And there is no power except with Allah. And we have devoted a book in some of our works on these verses, which suffices us from elaboration in this chapter.
Source pages p. 958

Commentary
This passage deals with another objection: if a madman or a child is not held accountable, then why should a major sinner be held accountable? Maturidi responds that the rulings for the madman and child are due to their lack of capacity, not because of iman. The major sinner, however, has iman, so his acts of worship are valid and counted. Then there is an objection that the sinner's prayer and fasting are to prevent an increase in sin, but the reply clarifies that these acts are only valid because of his iman. The shaykh (a later commentator) notes that the original questioner misunderstood; the acts are permitted because of iman alone.

Key Terms
المجنون
الصغير
حكم الإيمان
صلاة
صوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The major sinner's acts of worship are not valid because he is not fully accountable, similar to a madman or child.
Correction: The passage clarifies that worship is valid only due to the presence of faith, and that the rulings for madmen and children are due to lack of capacity, not lack of faith.

Checkpoint
According to the shaykh's clarification, what makes the prayer and fasting of a major sinner valid?
A) His fear of punishment
B) His faith
C) His lack of sin
D) His external circumstances

Key Takeaway
Acts of worship are valid only through faith, regardless of the performer's sinfulness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Maturidi's Refutation on Tahlil and the Insane/Minor
Maturidi answers an objection about tahlil involving the insane and minor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Maturidi's refutation of the objection that a fasiq's religious actions are valid only by virtue of others' faith, and why his own faith is essential.

Core Idea
A person's own faith, not another's, makes their religious actions valid and punishable.

Imam Maturidi here addresses a subtle objection: if an action like prayer is valid for a fasiq (grave sinner), could its validity depend on the faith of others, as with the insane and minors? Maturidi clarifies that this misunderstanding arises from not grasping the fundamental role of personal faith. Read on to see his decisive answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and initial response
First, read the objection that compares the fasiq to the insane and minor, and the initial response that the fasiq deserves the ruling by his own faith.
Notice how the objector uses analogy to challenge the role of personal faith.
2
Study the counter-objection about prayer
The objector then brings an example of prayer, suggesting it is valid for the fasiq. The initial answer says it is to prevent increase in fisq.
3
Focus on Maturidi's final clarification
Maturidi states that the questioner misunderstood. He explains that both actions are permissible only because of the person's own faith; without it, they would not be valid and there would be no punishment for abandoning them.
This is the core refutation: personal faith is necessary.
4
Connect to the broader principle
Reflect on how this defends the central role of personal belief in Islamic theology against those who would diminish it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفاسق ويصوم الإمام أبو منصور الماتريدي وعارض فى التحليل بالمجنون والصغير ، قيل : لها حكم الإيمان بغيرهما ؛ إذ لولا ذلك الغير لم يجب لها ذلك ، كما لو لم يجب لأولاد الكفرة وما نحن فيه لا غير فى ذلك يتبعه ، فثبت أنه استوجبه بإيمان نفسه . ثم عورض بما يصلى فقال : لئلا يزداد فسقه ويزول عنه عذاب تركها . قال الشيخ رحمه الله : يقال له : لم يفهم السؤال ، وإنما معنى ذلك أنهما يجوزان بالإيمان ، فلولا أنه مؤمن لم يكن ليمضيا له ويزيلا عذاب تركها عنه ، بل لا شيء عليه في تركها ، ولا يجوز أن يفعله لو لم يكن مؤمنا ، ولا قوة إلا بالله . وقد أفردنا في بعض كتبنا في هذه الآيات كتابا أغنانا عن الإطناب في هذا الباب
The fasiq (sinner) and fasting. Imam Abu Mansur al-Maturidi. And he objected in the matter of tahlil (declaring permissible) by [citing] the insane and the minor. It was said: They have the ruling of faith by virtue of others; because were it not for that other, it would not be incumbent upon them, just as it is not incumbent upon the children of the disbelievers, and what we are discussing follows nothing other than that in that regard. So it is established that he [the fasiq] deserved it [the ruling] by his own faith. Then he was objected to with regard to what he prayed, so he said: Lest his fisq (immorality) increase and the punishment of abandoning it be removed from him. The Shaykh (may Allah have mercy on him) said: It is said to him: He did not understand the question. Rather the meaning of that is that both [actions] are permissible by virtue of faith. Were he not a believer, they would not be carried out for him and the punishment of abandoning them would not be removed from him; rather, there is nothing upon him for abandoning them, and it is not permissible for him to do them if he were not a believer. And there is no power except through Allah. And we have devoted a book in some of our writings concerning these verses, which sufficed us from elaboration in this chapter.
Source pages p. 959

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer regarding the role of faith in the validity of certain actions. The objector argues that an action ruled permissible (tahlil) for the fasiq (grave sinner) is based on the faith of others, similar to how the insane and minors are ruled by the faith of others. Maturidi responds by clarifying that the questioner misunderstood: the actions are only permissible because of the person's own faith. Without faith, they would not be valid, and there would be no punishment for abandoning them. The passage then refers to a separate book for fuller treatment.

Key Terms
الفاسق (al-fāsiq)
التحليل (al-taḥlīl)
المجنون (al-majnūn)
الصغير (al-ṣaghīr)
الإيمان (al-īmān)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A fasiq's religious actions (like prayer) are valid because of the faith of others or because of their utilitarian benefit (e.g., preventing increase in sin).
Correction: Maturidi clarifies that all such actions are valid only due to the person's own faith. Without personal faith, the actions are not accepted and there is no punishment for abandoning them.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi's response, why are actions like prayer valid for a fasiq?
A) Because of the faith of others
B) Because of his own faith
C) Because he is compelled by law
D) Because he fears punishment

Key Takeaway
Maturidi emphasizes that without one's own faith, religious actions have no validity and carry no consequence, defending the centrality of personal belief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

If your personal faith were to weaken, would your prayers still be valid? What will you do today to strengthen your own faith, not just your outward practice?
?
The passage says that without personal faith, there is no punishment for abandoning acts—but also no reward. Are you content with performing rituals out of habit or social pressure, knowing they may count for nothing if your heart is not with them? What empty act will you transform into a sincere one this week?
?
When you sin, do you assume that as a Muslim you are still 'covered' by the faith of your parents or community? This lesson insists that only your own faith makes your actions count. Will you continue in complacency, or take responsibility for cultivating your iman as a matter of life and death?

The Good Deeds of the Believer Outweigh His Sins
Maturidi argues that the sinner's good deeds outweigh his sins, preventing eternal punishment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that a believer's good deeds outweigh his major sins (excluding shirk), preventing eternal punishment, and why this does not apply to disbelievers.

Core Idea
The believer's good deeds tip the scales against eternal punishment.

Maturidi presents the first of two ways to distinguish eternal from non-eternal punishment based on wisdom. He argues that a believer who commits major sins (excluding shirk) still performs acts of obedience that constitute good deeds, and these outweigh his sins. Therefore, eternal punishment would be unjust. This lesson unpacks that argument and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the logical flow: first a principle, then an application to the believer, then a contrast with the disbeliever.
The passage uses a conditional argument: if good deeds outweigh sins, then eternal punishment is unjust.
2
Identify the key distinction
Maturidi distinguishes between eternal and non-eternal punishment from the perspective of wisdom. Find where he states that punishment is based on what wisdom necessitates, not on arbitrary choice.
3
Understand the weighing argument
The believer's good deeds include fear of punishment, hope for mercy, trust in generosity, and withdrawal from wrath. Maturidi argues that these outweigh the evil of the sins, so depriving the believer of the benefit of those good deeds would be unjust.
This is the core of the argument.
4
Contrast with the disbeliever
Maturidi explicitly states that the disbeliever has no good deeds that can weigh against his sins. Therefore, eternal punishment for the disbeliever does not contradict generosity and nobility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم تذكر الفصل بين ما يخلد له العذاب ولا يخلد من طريق الحكمة وذلك يخرج على وجهين : أحدهما من طريق الاعتبار بتفاوت الذنوب في أنفسها وقد وعد الله أن لا يجزى إلا مثله ، وكذلك حق الحكمة ؛ إذ التعذيب يكون بما يوجبه الحكمة لا بما يختار ؛ إذ ليس هو نوع ما يختار وبخاصة ممن لا يضره الخلاف ، ثم هو الموصوف بالعفو والرحمة ، ولهذا ما أوجب المغفرة والعفو عن كثير من الذنوب . ثم يخرج ذا على وجوه : أحدها أنه ما أحد من يعصي الله بنوع من الكبائر دون الشرك إلا وهو لوقت العصيان مكتسب الطاعة من خوف عقاب ،] والنزع عن مقته ، ورجاء رحمته ، والثقة بكرمه ، / وذلك عن خيرات لو قوبل بها ما ارتكب من الخلاف بغلبة شهوة وقهر غضب أو نحو ذلك ليرجح ما كان له منه من خير على ما كان من شر ، فلا يجوز ، فلا يجوز أن يحرم نفع الخير ويوجب عقوبة الشر ، ومن ذلك فعله موصوف بالجود والكرم ولا كذلك معناهما ، ولا قوة إلا بالله . وليس مع من يكفر بالله ويشرك به معنى يستحق اسم الحسنة والخير ؛ لأنه يكذبه وينكر أمره ونهيه ، فلا يحتمل أن يكون له الرجا ، وفى دوام عذابه مضادة معنى الكرم والجود ، ولا قوة إلا بالله .
Then mention the distinction between that for which punishment is eternal and that which is not eternal from the way of wisdom. That is derived in two ways: One of them is by way of considering the disparity of sins in themselves, and Allah has promised that He will not requite except with its like, and such is the right of wisdom. For punishment comes from what wisdom necessitates, not from what one chooses, since it is not a type of choice, especially from one whom opposition does not harm. Then He is described with pardon and mercy, and because of that He has made forgiveness and pardon obligatory for many sins. Then this is derived in aspects: One of them is that there is no one who disobeys Allah by a kind of major sin except shirk, except that at the time of disobedience he is acquiring obedience from fear of punishment, and withdrawal from His wrath, and hope for His mercy, and trust in His generosity, and that is from good deeds which, if balanced against what he committed of opposition due to overwhelming desire or overpowering anger or the like, would outweigh the evil he has from it. So it is not permissible, it is not permissible to deprive him of the benefit of good and impose the punishment of evil. And because of that His action is described with generosity and nobility, but not so their meaning. And there is no power except through Allah. And there is not with the one who disbelieves in Allah and associates partners with Him any meaning that deserves the name of good deed or goodness, because he denies Him and rejects His command and prohibition. So it is not possible that he has hope, and in the permanence of his punishment there is contradiction to the meaning of generosity and nobility. And there is no power except through Allah.
Source pages p. 959

Commentary
This passage defines the first way of distinguishing eternal punishment from non-eternal punishment based on wisdom. The argument proceeds: Allah has promised proportionate requital, and wisdom dictates that punishment is based on necessity, not arbitrary choice. The sinner who commits major sins (excluding shirk) nonetheless performs acts of obedience (fear, hope, trust) which constitute good deeds. When balanced against his sins, these good deeds outweigh the evil. Therefore, it is not just to deprive him of the benefit of those good deeds and impose eternal punishment. In contrast, the disbeliever lacks any good deeds, so eternal punishment does not contradict generosity and nobility. The passage contrasts the two cases explicitly.

العذاب (al-ʿadhāb)
الحكمة (al-ḥikmah)
الكبائر (al-kabāʾir)
الشرك (al-shirk)
الحسنة (al-ḥasanah)
الجود (al-jūd)
الكرم (al-karam)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All major sins lead to eternal punishment regardless of the person's faith.
Correction: Maturidi shows that a believer's good deeds (fear, hope, trust) outweigh his major sins (excluding shirk), making eternal punishment unjust. Only disbelief warrants eternal punishment.

Checkpoint

D) The punishment is only for disbelievers

Key Takeaway
Maturidi argues that a believer's good deeds, motivated by fear and hope, outweigh his major sins, so eternal punishment is unjust; only disbelief warrants eternal punishment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you feel the fear of Allah's punishment and hope for His mercy? Or do you sin as if you are alone, forgetting that He sees you? This lesson says that even in your disobedience, these feelings are good deeds that outweigh your sin. But if you sin without any fear or hope, what does that say about your faith? Will you stop and check your heart the next time you are tempted?
?
Maturidi argues that a believer's good deeds outweigh his sins, but only if he has those good deeds from fear, hope, and trust. Have you relied on the mercy of Allah to excuse your sins without actually having that inner state? This lesson demands that you examine your motives. Are you truly hoping for His mercy or just taking it for granted? Commit today to recite His attributes and feel them before you act.
?
The disbeliever has no good deeds because he denies Allah. But what about you? Do you sometimes act as if Allah does not see you, even for a moment? That is a form of denial in action. This lesson warns that without the good deeds that come from acknowledging Allah, your deeds are empty. Spend a minute now asking Allah to fill your heart with fear and hope so that your actions are weighed in your favor.

Second Way: Proportionate Requital for Disbelief
Maturidi argues that eternal punishment is proportionate to the infinite gravity of disbelief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that eternal punishment for disbelief is proportionate because shirk is the greatest sin and the disbeliever has no good deeds to offset it.

Core Idea
Shirk's infinite gravity demands eternal punishment; no good deeds exist to balance it.

Maturidi now presents a second argument for why disbelief leads to eternal punishment. He grounds it in the principle of proportionate requital: Allah's justice requires that punishment fit the crime. Since shirk is the greatest sin and the disbeliever has no good deeds at all, the only just requital is eternity in the Fire. This lesson unpacks that careful reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Maturidi builds his case: from Allah's promise, to the gravity of shirk, to the absence of good deeds, then to the conclusion of eternity.
Pay attention to the logical connectors: 'ف' (so) and 'إذ' (for).
2
Identify the key premises
List out the premises Maturidi uses: Allah's promise of proportionate requital, shirk being the greatest sin in reason, the disbeliever having no good deeds, and the thought experiment about accepting multiple punishments for one day of salvation.
Each premise supports the conclusion that eternal punishment is just.
3
Understand the supporting argument
Maturidi strengthens his case by noting that a disbeliever would accept far greater punishment for just one day of relief. This shows that the intended punishment (eternity) is actually what is due.
This step uses a hypothetical to illustrate proportionality.
4
Contrast with lesser sins
Maturidi then distinguishes disbelievers from those who commit lesser sins but have good deeds. He concludes that for disbelievers, with no good deeds, only eternity suffices.
This contrast clarifies that the argument is specific to shirk.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني أن الله جل ثناؤه وعدا أن لا يجزى إلا مثلها ، ومثل الشرك الذي في العقل أكبر من كل ذنب ، مع ما لا حسنة يكون معه ومع غيره ، إنما هو الخلود في النار ؛ إذ معلوم أن الكافر يرضى بأضعاف ما يعذب مع النجاة يوما من الدهر ، فيبين ذلك أن تمام جزائه الخلود ، فإذا كان لغيره مثله فيجزى غيره أكثر من مثل الفعل ، وذلك جوار فى حكمته ، والله يجل عنه ، فهذا ما كان مرتكب ما دونه حسنات، وليس معه ، ولا قوة إلا بالله
And the second: That Allah, may His glory be exalted, promised that He will not requite except with its like. And the like of shirk (associating partners), which in reason is greater than every sin, together with the fact that there is no good deed with him nor with others, is only eternity in the Fire. For it is known that the disbeliever would accept multiple times what he is punished with along with salvation for one day of time. This shows that his complete requital is eternity. If another had his like, then he [the other] would be requited with more than the like of the act, and that is injustice in His wisdom, and Allah is exalted above that. So this is what one who commits what is less than it has good deeds, and he has none. And there is no power except through Allah.
Source pages p. 960

Commentary
This passage presents the second way of distinguishing eternal punishment: the principle of proportionate requital. Maturidi argues that shirk is the greatest sin in reason, and the disbeliever has no good deeds. Therefore, the only just requital for shirk is eternal punishment in the Fire. If someone else committed a sin of equal magnitude (which is impossible except for another disbeliever), they would receive more than the like, which would be unjust. So the complete requital for disbelief is eternity, while those who commit lesser sins have good deeds to mitigate. The argument relies on the proportionality and the absence of good deeds for the disbeliever.

الشرك (al-shirk)
الخلود (al-khulūd)
الجزاء (al-jazāʾ)
المثل (al-mithl)
الحسنة (al-ḥasanah)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any major sin could warrant eternal punishment.
Correction: Maturidi specifies that only shirk has no good deeds and is the greatest sin in reason, so eternal punishment is unique to disbelief.

Checkpoint

A) Because shirk is the only sin
B) Because shirk has no like and the disbeliever has no good deeds
C) Because Allah is angry with disbelievers
D) Because the disbeliever will not repent

Key Takeaway
Eternal punishment for disbelief is just because shirk is the greatest sin and the disbeliever has no good deeds to mitigate it, making eternity the only proportionate requital.

?
Allah's justice is perfectly proportionate, as Maturidi argues. Is there an area of your life where you secretly doubt that Allah's judgment is fair? How can you actively submit your heart to His wisdom, even when you don't understand your trials?

The Expiation Argument: Hudud and Eternal Punishment
Maturidi uses hudud as expiations to argue for eternal punishment of disbelief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the expiatory nature of legal punishments (hudud) to argue for the eternal punishment of disbelief.

Core Idea
If hudud are expiations, disbelief must have no expiation, so its punishment is eternal.

Maturidi presents a logical argument from the nature of legal punishments (hudud) in Islam. He observes that these punishments serve as expiations for sins. This leads to a key insight about disbelief: since there is no expiation for disbelief in this world, its punishment must be eternal, unlike other sins which have limited punishment.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the logical flow from premise to conclusion.
The argument uses conditional reasoning: if not expiation, then absurd.
2
Identify the key premise
Notice that Maturidi states that the hudud are made expiations for sins. This is the foundational point.
This premise is taken from Islamic law and tradition.
3
Follow the conditional reasoning
Consider the hypothetical: if hudud did not expiate, they would increase punishment for disbelief, which is impossible because lesser sins cannot have greater punishment.
The impossibility shows that hudud must be expiations.
4
Understand the conclusion about disbelief
Since there is no expiation for disbelief in this world, its punishment cannot be limited. Therefore, it is eternal.
This contrasts with other sins which have a limited punishment (hadd).
5
Reflect on the broader implication
Consider how this argument reinforces the severity of disbelief compared to other sins.
Maturidi uses this to support the doctrine of eternal punishment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا أن الحدود في الدنيا جعلن كفارات لما يرتكب من الذنوب ، فلو لم يكن فيها تكفير كانت تكون زيادات على عقوبات الكفر ، ومحال أن يزداد عقوبة ما دون الكفر ، فثبت أنها كفارات ، ولا كفارة للكفر في الدنيا ، ثبت أنه لا يحتمل في العقوبة فجعلت عقوبته أبدية وعقوبة غيره بحد ، فكذلك العقوبة الموعودة فيه ، ولا قوة إلا بالله
And also that the legal punishments (ḥudūd) in this world are made expiations (kaffārāt) for sins that are committed. For if they did not involve expiation, they would be additions to the punishments of disbelief, and it is impossible that the punishment for what is less than disbelief increases. So it is established that they are expiations, and there is no expiation for disbelief in this world. It is established that it [disbelief] does not bear [limited] punishment, so its punishment is made eternal, and the punishment of other than it is [by] a limit. And likewise is the promised punishment in it. And there is no power except through Allah.
Source pages p. 960

Commentary
This passage presents a proof from the legal punishments (ḥudūd) in Islamic law. Maturidi argues that these punishments serve as expiations for sins. If they did not expiate, they would be additional punishments for the disbeliever, which is absurd because punishments for lesser sins cannot exceed those for disbelief. Therefore, the ḥudūd are expiations. However, there is no expiation for disbelief in this world, so the punishment for disbelief must be eternal. This contrasts with other sins which have a limited punishment (the ḥadd) in this world, implying a limited punishment in the Hereafter as well. Thus, the promised punishment for disbelief is eternal.

Key Terms
الحدود (al-ḥudūd)
الكفارات (al-kaffārāt)
الكفر (al-kufr)
العقوبة (al-ʿuqūbah)
الأبدية (al-abadiyyah)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that ḥudūd are purely punitive, with no expiatory function.
Correction: Maturidi argues that ḥudūd are expiations; otherwise they would absurdly increase punishment for disbelief.

Checkpoint
According to this argument, why is disbelief punished eternally while other sins have limited punishment?
A) Because disbelief is the only sin without expiation in this world
B) Because disbelievers never repent
C) Because ḥudūd are not applied to disbelievers
D) Because Allah is more severe with disbelievers

Key Takeaway
The expiatory function of hudud implies that disbelief, having no expiation, must be punished eternally.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If the hudud are expiatory, what does that say about God's mercy and justice? Have you ever felt that God's punishments in this life are harsh, or do you now see them as a blessing that spares you from greater punishment? How will this change the way you respond to trials?

Double Punishment for Misleaders Only
Maturidi refutes the idea that all major sins double punishment, using Quranic specificity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that only leading others astray, not committing major sins in general, carries a doubled punishment.

Core Idea
Doubling of punishment is specific to misguidance, not to every major sin.

Maturidi presents a crucial distinction between the punishment for disbelief itself and the punishment for additional sins. Using the Quranic promise of double punishment for those who disbelieve and mislead, he argues that major sins do not automatically double one’s punishment. This distinction protects the Islamic understanding of justice and the specificity of divine threats.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the Quranic quotes and the logical flow of Maturidi's reasoning.
Focus on the conditional 'if...then' structure in the middle.
2
Identify the key claim and evidence
Maturidi states that Allah promises double punishment to those who disbelieve and mislead others. Then he argues: if major sins doubled punishment, every disbeliever would get double; but Allah specifically singles out misleaders.
The Quranic verses (29:13 and 7:38) are his evidence for specificity.
3
Understand the conclusion
Maturidi concludes that major sins do not automatically cause doubling. He also contrasts the punishment of disbelievers (all sins) with that of believers (not all sins).
This reinforces that disbelief is the foundation of eternal punishment, while lesser sins are treated differently.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا أن الله جل ثناؤه أخبر أن الموعودة عقوبة الذين كفروا وأضلوا غيرهم ضعف عقوبة من كفر ولم يضل غيره ، ثم لو كان للكافر عقوبة غير الإضلال مثل عقوبة الإضلال لكان كل كافر عقوبته مضاعفة ؛ لأنه لا كافر إلا ومعه سوى الكفر - كبائر ، وقد خص الله بالمضاعفة المضلين بقوله : وَلَيَحْمِلُنَّ أَثْقَالَهُمْ وَأَثْقَالًا مَعَ أَنْقَالِهِم ، وقول الأتباع : رَبَّنَا هَوْلَاء أَضَلُّونَا ، وجعل لكل ضعفا ، فبطل أن يكون ذلك عقوبة الكبيرة ، بل هي لو كانت في الكفر كان أحق للضعف منه فى الإسلام للمثل ، ألا ترى أنه يعاقب الكافر بجميع الآثام من صغائر وكبائر ، ولا كذلك أمر من اعتقد دين الإسلام ، ولا قوة الا بالله
And also that Allah, may His glory be exalted, informed that the promised punishment of those who disbelieve and mislead others is double the punishment of one who disbelieves but does not mislead others. Then, if the disbeliever had a punishment other than misguidance like the punishment for misguidance, then every disbeliever would have a doubled punishment, because there is no disbeliever except that with him besides disbelief there are major sins. And Allah specifically singled out the misleaders for doubling by His saying: 'They will certainly bear their own burdens and [other] burdens along with their burdens' (29:13), and the saying of the followers: 'Our Lord, these are they who misled us' (7:38), and He made for each a double. So it is invalid that that is a punishment for the major sin. Rather, if it [the major sin] were in disbelief, it would be more deserving of doubling than in Islam for the like. Do you not see that He punishes the disbeliever for all sins, small and great, but not so for the one who professes the religion of Islam. And there is no power except through Allah.
Source pages p. 960

Commentary
This passage presents a proof from the Quran. Allah states that those who disbelieve and mislead others receive double punishment. If major sins (other than disbelief) also carried a doubled punishment, then every disbeliever would have double punishment because they all commit major sins. But Allah specifically attributes doubling to misleaders, not to all disbelievers. Therefore, major sins do not automatically double the punishment. The disbeliever is punished for all his sins (major and minor), but the believer who commits major sins is not punished for every sin in the same way. This reinforces that the punishment for disbelief is eternal and distinct, while lesser sins have different treatment.

Key Terms
المضاعفة (al-muḍāʿafah)
الإضلال (al-iḍlāl)
الكبائر (al-kabāʾir)
الصغائر (al-ṣaghāʾir)
الأثقال (al-athqāl)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that all major sins are punished with double the punishment in the afterlife.
Correction: Maturidi shows from the Quran that double punishment is specifically for those who disbelieve and mislead others, not for mere major sins.

Checkpoint
What does the Quranic verse about double punishment for misleaders prove according to Maturidi?
A) That major sins always double punishment
B) That only misguidance doubles punishment, not major sins in general
C) That all disbelievers get double punishment
D) That believers are not punished for sins

Key Takeaway
Maturidi proves that only leading others astray, not committing major sins in general, results in doubled punishment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Think of a person you influence—a child, a friend, a colleague. Is there any habit or word you use that could subtly lead them toward sin or away from faith? What concrete change will you make this week to ensure you are a guide to good, not a misleader?

If you have ever led someone astray, even unwittingly, does the thought of bearing their burdens along with your own terrify you? What step of repentance or rectification have you been postponing? Today, write it down and take the first action.

Second Argument: God's Promise of Just Recompense
Maturidi presents a proof from God's promise of exact recompense for kufr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's proof that kufr deserves eternal punishment based on God's promise of exact recompense and the unique severity of shirk.

Core Idea
Shirk's unique enormity demands eternal punishment, as no good deeds can counterbalance it.

In this passage, Imam al-Maturidi presents his second argument for the eternal punishment of disbelief. He draws on God's promise to requite every deed justly and argues that shirk is so severe that no good deed can accompany it, so its only just recompense is eternity in the Fire. Let's examine the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own logic come through.
The passage is a single continuous argument.
2
Identify the key premise
Notice that the argument starts with God's promise to requite every deed with its like. This is the foundational premise.
3
Understand why shirk is unique
The author claims that shirk is the greatest sin and that no good deed can accompany it. Consider why this is so in Islamic theology.
Shirk nullifies all good deeds, leaving no counterbalance.
4
Follow the supporting reasoning
The author uses a hypothetical: a disbeliever would accept multiplied punishment in exchange for one day of salvation. This illustrates that the only just recompense for kufr is eternity.
5
Grasp the conclusion
The final sentence contrasts the kafir with committers of lesser sins, who may have good deeds. The kafir has none, so his punishment is eternal.
This argument distinguishes kufr from other sins.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني أن الله جل ثناؤه وعدا أن لا يجزى إلا مثلها ، ومثل الشرك الذي في العقل أكبر من كل ذنب ، مع ما لا حسنة يكون معه ومع غيره ، إنما هو الخلود في النار ؛ إذ معلوم أن الكافر يرضى بأضعاف ما يعذب مع النجاة يوما من الدهر ، فيبين ذلك أن تمام جزائه الخلود ، فإذا كان لغيره مثله فيجزى غيره أكثر من مثل الفعل ، وذلك جوار فى حكمته ، والله يجل عنه ، فهذا ما كان مرتكب ما دونه حسنات، وليس معه ، ولا قوة إلا بالله
And the second: that God, magnificent is His praise, promised that He will not requite except with its like, and the likeness of shirk in the intellect is greater than every sin, along with which no good deed can be with it or with other than it; it is but eternal abiding in the Fire. For it is known that the disbeliever would accept multiple times the punishment he suffers along with salvation for a day of time, so that shows that the completion of his requital is eternity. If another had the like of it, then another would be requited with more than the like of the act, and that is injustice in His wisdom, and God is exalted above that. So this is the state of the committer of what is less than it—good deeds, and not with it—and there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 960

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument (following an unstated first) for the eternal punishment of kufr. It is a proof from God's promise of exact recompense. The author states that God promised to requite each deed with its like. The likeness of shirk (associating partners with God) in the intellect is greater than any sin, and no good deed can accompany it or be with anything other than it—meaning shirk nullifies all good deeds. Therefore its requital is eternal abiding in the Fire. The proof proceeds by noting that a disbeliever would willingly accept multiplied punishment in exchange for even one day of salvation, which shows that the full requital must be eternal; if another sin had the same weight, the punishment would exceed the act's due, implying injustice in God's wisdom—which is impossible. Thus the committer of lesser sins (below kufr) may have good deeds, but the kafir has none, so his punishment is eternal.

Key Terms
الخلود
الجزاء
الشرك
الكافر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's promise of exact recompense implies that all sins, including shirk, are punished proportionally and not necessarily eternally.
Correction: Maturidi argues that shirk is uniquely severe because no good deeds can accompany it, so its just recompense is eternal, unlike lesser sins which may be balanced by good deeds.

Checkpoint

A) Because God promised to requite every deed exactly, and shirk is the greatest sin with no accompanying good deeds.
B) Because the disbeliever refuses to repent.
C) Because God is angry with the disbeliever.
D) Because the disbeliever's evil deeds outweigh his good deeds.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi argues that kufr deserves eternal punishment because shirk is the gravest sin, with no possible good deeds to counterbalance it, and God's justice requires exact recompense.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

What subtle forms of shirk in your own life have you tolerated as 'minor'? Will you commit this week to identifying and repenting from them with the same urgency as if they were the greatest sin?

Maturidi argues that God's justice requires exactly matching punishment. If you truly believe God will requite every deed with its like, how does that change the way you evaluate your own actions today?
